    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_656688410" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/656688410" />
    <a title="ReemK10 (Paper Pills)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15726428-reemk10-paper-pills"><img alt="ReemK10 (Paper Pills)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507553429p2/15726428.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/656688410?book_show_action=true">Jul 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ReemK10 (Paper Pills)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ReemK10 (Paper Pills)" href="/user/show/15726428-reemk10-paper-pills">ReemK10 (Paper Pills)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781409041580" href="/book/show/17427584-in-search-of-lost-time-vol-4">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer656688410" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17881103493106779984">"Flower and plant have no conscious will. They are shameless, exposing their genitals. And so in a sense are Proust's men and women .... shameless. There is no sense of right and wrong." Samuel Beckett<br/><br/><br/> Marcel Proust writes: "People in society are too apt to think of a book as a sort of cube one side of which has been removed so the author can " put in" the people he meets."(MKE 90) He goes on to say that it is " thanks to them when one reads a book or an article, one "gets to know the inside sto</span>
  <span id="freeText17881103493106779984" style="display:none">"Flower and plant have no conscious will. They are shameless, exposing their genitals. And so in a sense are Proust's men and women .... shameless. There is no sense of right and wrong." Samuel Beckett<br /><br /><br /> Marcel Proust writes: "People in society are too apt to think of a book as a sort of cube one side of which has been removed so the author can " put in" the people he meets."(MKE 90) He goes on to say that it is " thanks to them when one reads a book or an article, one "gets to know the inside story " one" sees people in their true colours." (MKE 90) Proust is such an author. His characters may be viewed as being shameless because they have been exposed to reveal their true identities. Readers acquire the botanist's technique of contemplating and observing "the precious plants exposed in the courtyard." Seeing the laws of the vegetable kingdom command the germination of a flower, one also observes the laws of society mandating the behavior of people, namely their pleasures.<br /><br /> Unveiling people's true natures in turn resonates and touches a responsive chord within us making us aware of vices that are laid bare. Proust explains opening our eyes to what was invisible to us before. Everything becomes clear. We arrive at conclusions that we never forget. Social constraints, inward constraints impose themselves and are observed. Proust does not let us suffer the disillusionment of deception, even as we guess at them. Proust delivers as the reader acquires a new way of seeing things- the exquisite blue girandole of a jellyfish makes us now see a mauve orchid of the sea. Proust intends for us to become these human herbalists and moral botanists as we observe the shameless exposition of flower and plant in whatever metaphorical forms and to notice the flowers that mimic the insects that they wish to attract. <br /><br /> Love of ostentation will strike the reader as the shameless behavior of polite society as is self-importance and social categorization.Proust has us realizing that getting to know people better makes them change before our own eyes with displays that can often be most infuriating. Previously, seeing through non- transparent glass has us seeing in distortion. Utilizing Proust's different degrees of attention has our eyes dilating and bringing us to a new reality. <br /><br /> Proust teaches that it is love that magnifies our perception of people's traits. Faults are often seen as superior qualities.We also encounter rude awakenings when we recognize parts of ourselves we'd rather not see in those people who bear a striking resemblance to who we are.<br /><br /> Beckett's claim that the characters of the Recherche are shameless is an interesting one because any flawed personality is bound to feel shame. Perhaps Proust's characters do not feel any guilt for their actions, and so for Beckett that meant that "There is no question of right and wrong." It is true that Proust doesn't expose his characters to a shameful glance and that might make them be perceived as being shameless. Proust and Beckett are clearly at opposite ends. Proust is obsessed with time, whereas with Beckett there is no sense of time. Proust is in search of involuntary memory, whereas with Beckett there is no memory. Proust has an extensive cast of characters, whereas Beckett has them stripped down to the bare minimum.<br /><br /> Proust presents a moral subversion of his characters that we come to accept them for what they are, as ugly, ridiculous, obsessive, or neurotic as they may be. His are not characters that necessarily inspire empathy, nor does he judge them. Proust only presents social interaction and leaves the reader to figure out what the flaws are in their personalities as he adds layer upon layer of jealousy, rivalry, and envy. There is intimating with eyes, making gratitudinous gestures, tilting against windmills, and eleventh- hour reviews of the troops invited to a garden party. The favorable becomes unfavorable and vice versa.<br /> <br /> Proust has us understanding that there can be no shame because one can only be what one is. "The question is not, as for Hamlet, to be or not to be, but to belong or not to belong." (MKE 572)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17881103493106779984" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1887412851" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1887412851" />
    <a title="Ian Scuffling" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22428213-ian-scuffling"><img alt="Ian Scuffling" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1482102664p2/22428213.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1887412851?book_show_action=true">Mar 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian Scuffling" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian Scuffling" href="/user/show/22428213-ian-scuffling">Ian Scuffling</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375753107" href="/book/show/35750.Sodom_and_Gomorrah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22428213-ian-scuffling?shelf=read-in-2017">read-in-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22428213-ian-scuffling?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22428213-ian-scuffling?shelf=print-aint-dead">print-aint-dead</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22428213-ian-scuffling?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1887412851" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13875008495645936633">What does it mean to read Proust? Is it to embrace the claustrophobic, monomaniacal prose as if it were normal and beautiful, or to recognize its neurotic and dangerously self-obsessed presentation? Marcel the narrator is not an okay man. He's an insane narcissist. And an overwhelming egotist--dozens of other characters exist on the page, but it's as if they're only Marcel himself, with a kind of Cartesian solipsism that wears down the reader's trust, sentence-by-sentence, word-by-word. There's </span>
  <span id="freeText13875008495645936633" style="display:none">What does it mean to read Proust? Is it to embrace the claustrophobic, monomaniacal prose as if it were normal and beautiful, or to recognize its neurotic and dangerously self-obsessed presentation? Marcel the narrator is not an okay man. He's an insane narcissist. And an overwhelming egotist--dozens of other characters exist on the page, but it's as if they're only Marcel himself, with a kind of Cartesian solipsism that wears down the reader's trust, sentence-by-sentence, word-by-word. There's no trusting him for he's dictated strictly by whims and airs, and so are his impressions of those around him. Marcel himself is but a pasteboard to hide the absence of empathy or even critical self-awareness. The way in which he is over-wrought in his style is a cover, hiding the abscess of his character.<br /><br />Is to read Proust a pleasure? I'm not convinced. The language washes over the event, the characters, the thoughts. It ruins engagement beyond the prose, which overwhelms in a way that subverts its own pleasures (I'm willing to let <i>some</i> of this go to the fact that I've read 4 volumes in 3 months). I find nuggets popping up giving glimmers of a reminder of what it was like to read Vols. 1 &amp; 2 (oh the joy in those two books!), but the society scenes are just so patently boring. One may argue that the beauty in Proust's prose makes up for the unrelenting boredom of the scene, but it seems more an excuse for Proust to show off how even the mundane can be beautiful while being too distracted to make any of it beautiful. It's the irony of the word pulchritude--such an hideous word to describe such beauty, but to use the word itself is somehow beautiful. Perhaps these qualms have always been my problem with late-19th and early-20th century Realism? Maybe I'm just not a Proust reader? Fair enough.<br /><br />I know I'm in the minority here, but Proust's flowery prose is verging on prolix, only adding to the boredom of the story and the characters. An on going test of pleasure and principle. I'm determined to finish ISOLT, but I must take a break for now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13875008495645936633" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer16396728530419282968">I don't need to detail the plot it meanders on as beautifully written as ever with the Guermantes and Verdurins being wonderfully silly as ever. The narrator continues to pursue and Albertine and get bored of her. The minor characters stand out in this volume; Charlus, Morel and the members of the salons. <br/>Proust explores homosexuality in some detail; both male and female. Of course the Proust scholars continue to debate the real nature of the narrator's lovers; Albertine, Andree and Gilberte are</span>
  <span id="freeText16396728530419282968" style="display:none">I don't need to detail the plot it meanders on as beautifully written as ever with the Guermantes and Verdurins being wonderfully silly as ever. The narrator continues to pursue and Albertine and get bored of her. The minor characters stand out in this volume; Charlus, Morel and the members of the salons. <br />Proust explores homosexuality in some detail; both male and female. Of course the Proust scholars continue to debate the real nature of the narrator's lovers; Albertine, Andree and Gilberte are all feminised forms of male names. <br />Proust also makes interesting observations and reflections on death, grief and ageing. The narrator lost his grandmother in the previous volume and he continues to feel her loss and dream about her. There is also a moment of revelation when he suddenly sees his mother as his grandmother; time is passing. I don't really feel the need to over analyse at this point because I'm just enjoying the journey; it's like falling into an impressionist painting</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_661088315" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/661088315" />
    <a title="Margaret" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5138376-margaret"><img alt="Margaret" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1377398332p2/5138376.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/661088315?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Margaret" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Margaret" href="/user/show/5138376-margaret">Margaret</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375753107" href="/book/show/35750.Sodom_and_Gomorrah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5138376-margaret?shelf=proust-2013">proust-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5138376-margaret?shelf=translation">translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5138376-margaret?shelf=read-summer-2013">read-summer-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5138376-margaret?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5138376-margaret?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5138376-margaret?shelf=20th-century-literature">20th-century-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer661088315" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1590800258981985552"><blockquote>
  <i>Two or three times it occurred to me, for a moment, that the world in which this room and these bookshelves were situated, and in which Albertine counted for so little, was perhaps an intellectual world, which was the sole reality, and my grief something like what we fell when we read a novel, a thing of which only a madman would make a lasting and permanent grief that prolonged itself through his life; that a tiny flicker of my will would suffice, perhaps, to attain to this real world, to re</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText1590800258981985552" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i>Two or three times it occurred to me, for a moment, that the world in which this room and these bookshelves were situated, and in which Albertine counted for so little, was perhaps an intellectual world, which was the sole reality, and my grief something like what we fell when we read a novel, a thing of which only a madman would make a lasting and permanent grief that prolonged itself through his life; that a tiny flicker of my will would suffice, perhaps, to attain to this real world, to re-enter it by breaking through my grief as one breaks through a paper hoop, and to think no more about what Albertine had done than we think about the actions of the imaginary heroine of a novel after we have finished reading. (718) </i>
</blockquote><br /><br />From the opening scene about flowers and gay men to the end, where the narrator appears to have Albertine at last, this is the beginning of the narrator in love with actual women instead of books and memories. He's also exposed to love in terms of the death of his grandmother and his mourning mother, who persists in keeping up some habits like reading the letters of Mme. Sévigné in order to remember. It's memory with a tinge of grief and memory outside of the Narrator's experience, which makes his observation of it even more interesting. For as much as he is struggling with memories, others around him are too, though with his mother it appears maybe the strongest. <br /><br />Classical music, too, takes a large role, both as a proof of a social standing but also because of some deeply individual and interesting relationship between each person and the musician. This also counts double since the violinist is having an affair with a man, which takes us to our next major theme. <br /><br />For all the Proustian meditations on memory, grief, and art, this volume of <i>In Search of Lost Time</i> is very much the "narrator discovers gay people" one. I don't want to dwell on this too much because it constantly feels like I am trying to put some sort of modern sensibly on the whole thing, which is so much different than the previous cause of the wealthy set, the Dreyfus affair, but on the other hand the homosexuality issue becomes more important in <i>The Captive and the Fugitive</i>. There have been hints of it all along, for example the woman in Combray who clearly has a girlfriend, but for once the narrator must encounter those who might not have a standard heterosexual private life on much closer terms than before. The gossip turns into something more personal, as is the way with our narrator, and how this develops over time is something that leaks into the next volume so I'll discuss it more there. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[But really, Narrator, you can have all the sassy gay friends you want but please deal with your issues when you want to marry one because if she is a lesbian, as you keep suspecting, things might not work out. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Finally, in a moment of pure hilarity, the Narrator first encounters an airplane and the general panic it instills reminds me that however modern Proust sounds, there are still some experiences that remain outside the realm of the modern reader. <br /><br />So, onwards to <i>The Captive and the Fugitive</i>!<br /><br /><br /><br />------Initial thoughts--------<br />I'm going to need a while to let the review stew in my brain. Until then, some first impressions on finishing it:<br /><br />So much to think about and consider in terms of not just parties but life, love, and relationships <br /><br />The narrator's mother and descriptions of grief are beautifully truthful<br /><br />Why yes, Mr. Narrator, gay people do exist. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Marrying your gay best friend might not be the solution  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I laughed out loud when the Narrator was surprised by an airplane flying overhead. <br /><br />I love the last 40 pages or so. They were absolutely worth it. <br /><br />Also, sometimes a 3 week break from a reading project can be helpful, because I really enjoyed finishing <i>Sodom and Gomorrah</i> instead of rushing through to stay on schedule. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1590800258981985552" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1984845621" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1984845621" />
    <a title="Greg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39346323-greg"><img alt="Greg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513962691p2/39346323.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1984845621?book_show_action=true">Apr 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg" href="/user/show/39346323-greg">Greg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375753107" href="/book/show/35750.Sodom_and_Gomorrah">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39346323-greg?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39346323-greg?shelf=translation">translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39346323-greg?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1984845621" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15316162938667780753">"The women shall have Gomorrah and the men shall have Sodom" opens this book (the quote is by Alfred De Vigny). And Proust certainly delivers, surprisingly (given the publication year of 1921) covering many points along the sexual spectrum. He writes that in the past he has found himself "...stupidly thinking that people could have but one sort of love." But that's just one of three main plot lines: the second is the Dreyfus affair, and society is far more passionate about Dreyfus than about sex</span>
  <span id="freeText15316162938667780753" style="display:none">"The women shall have Gomorrah and the men shall have Sodom" opens this book (the quote is by Alfred De Vigny). And Proust certainly delivers, surprisingly (given the publication year of 1921) covering many points along the sexual spectrum. He writes that in the past he has found himself "...stupidly thinking that people could have but one sort of love." But that's just one of three main plot lines: the second is the Dreyfus affair, and society is far more passionate about Dreyfus than about sexual preferences. And the third plot line is, of course, time: "...my effort to wake up consisted chiefly in an effort to bring the obscure, undefined mass of the sleep in which I had just been living into the framework of time." Proust beautifully weaves these 3 plot lines of duality together: Sodom/Gomorrah, Dreyfus/anti-Dreyfuss, and Sleep/Wake. And to top it all off, the author leaves us begging for more, as he ends this volume with a great cliff-hanger which is the only climax of this type (it seems to me) in the first four volumes. This is the final volume of "In Search of Lost Time" published within the author's own lifetime: the remaining 3 volumes were published posthumously. But Proust had achieved international fame with the publication of the second volume and I believe he embraced that fame to produce what is turning out to be, so far, one of my favorite literary works. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15316162938667780753" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    }
  }

  }

Not only are there frequent references to specific works of art, but certain characters are also evoked by comparison to particular paintings. Bloch’s appearance as a boy is likened to the portrait of Mehmet II by Gentile Bellini; Odette de Crécy strikes Swann by her resemblance to a figure in a Botticelli fresco. Even the lesser figure of a certain Mme. Blattin becomes the subject of Proustian mischief by being described as “exactly the portrait of Savonarola by Fra Bartolomeo.” Eric Karpeles has identified and located the many paintings to which Proust makes reference and sets them alongside the relevant text from the novel; in other cases, where only a painter’s name is mentioned to indicate a certain style or appearance, Karpeles has chosen a representative work to illustrate the impression that Proust sought to evoke.\n\n\nWith some 200 paintings beautifully reproduced in full color and texts drawn from the Moncrieff/Kilmartin/Enright translation, as well as concise commentaries on the evolving narrative, this book is an essential addition to the libraries of Proustians everywhere.       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_40378238'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40378238-lost-time?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Lost Time: Lectures on Proust in a Soviet Prison Camp<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/3351923.J_zef_Czapski?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Józef Czapski<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.08 avg rating &mdash; 309 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1948\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer13495415451377082962\">During the Second World War, as a prisoner of war in a Soviet camp, and with nothing but memory to go on, the Polish artist and soldier Józef Czapski brought Marcel Proust’s In Search of Lost Time to life for an audience of prison inmates. In a serie<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText13495415451377082962\" style=\"display:none\">During the Second World War, as a prisoner of war in a Soviet camp, and with nothing but memory to go on, the Polish artist and soldier Józef Czapski brought Marcel Proust’s In Search of Lost Time to life for an audience of prison inmates. In a series of lectures, Czapski described the arc and import of Proust’s masterpiece, sketched major and minor characters in striking detail, and movingly evoked the work’s originality, depth, and beauty. Eric Karpeles has translated this brilliant and ­altogether unparalleled feat of the critical imagination into English for the first time, and in a thoughtful introduction he brings out how, in reckoning with Proust’s great meditation on memory, Czapski helped his fellow officers to remember that there was a world apart from the world of the camp. Proust had staked the art of the novelist against the losses of a lifetime and the imminence of death. Recalling that triumphant wager, unfolding, like Sheherazade, the intricacies of Proust’s world night after night, Czapski showed to men at the end of their tether that the past remained present and there was a future in which to hope.\n\nIncludes an 8-page color insert of Czapski’s lecture notes.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13495415451377082962\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; In a remarkable instance of literary and philosophical interpretation, the incomparable Gilles Deleuze reads Proust\'s work as a narrative of an apprenticeship-more precisely, the apprenticeship of a man of letters. Considering the search as one directed by an experience of signs, in which the protagonist learns to interpret and decode the kinds and types of symbols that surround him, Deleuze conducts us on a corollary search-one that leads to a new and deeper understanding of the signs that constitute A la recherche du temps perdu..\nDeleuze traces the network of signs laid by Proust (those of love, art, or worldliness) and moves toward an aesthetics that culminates in a meditation on the literary work as a sign-producing &quot;machine&quot;-an operation that reveals the superiority of &quot;signs of art&quot; in a world of signs.\nIn Richard Howard\'s graceful translation, augmented with an essay that Deleuze added to a later French edition, Proust and Signs appears here for the first time in its entirety in English. Admired in its original appearance as an imaginative and innovative study of Proust and as one of Deleuze\'s more accessible works, Proust and Signs stands as the writer\'s most sustained attempt to understand and explain the work of art. For what it reveals about both Deleuze and his subject, it remains a source of literary and philosophical insight, inspiration, and surpassing interest.\nGilles Deleuze (1925-1995) was professor of philosophy at the University of Paris, Vincennes-St. Denis. With Félix Guattari, he coauthored Anti-Oedipus (1983) and A Thousand Plateaus (1987). Among his other works are Cinema 1 (1986), Cinema 2 (1989), Foucault (1988), The Fold (1992), and Essays Critical and Clinical (1997), all published by the University of Minnesota Press. \nRichard Howard recently translated The Charterhouse of Parma by Stendhal for the Modern Library and has also translated works by Barthes, Foucault, and Todorov.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/233619.Marcel_Proust">About Marcel Proust</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/233619.Marcel_Proust"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1392271688p3/233619.jpg);'></div>

<a href="/author/show/233619.Marcel_Proust">Marcel Proust

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10303726357976908525">French novelist, best known for his 3000 page masterpiece <i>À la recherche du temps perdu</i> (<i>Remembrance of Things Past</i> or <i>In Search of Lost Time</i>), a pseudo-autobiographical novel told mostly in a stream-of-consciousness style. <br/><br/>Born in the first year of the Third Republic, the young Marcel, like his narrator, was a delicate child from a bourgeois family. He was active in Parisian high society during t</span>
  <span id="freeText10303726357976908525" style="display:none">French novelist, best known for his 3000 page masterpiece <i>À la recherche du temps perdu</i> (<i>Remembrance of Things Past</i> or <i>In Search of Lost Time</i>), a pseudo-autobiographical novel told mostly in a stream-of-consciousness style. <br /><br />Born in the first year of the Third Republic, the young Marcel, like his narrator, was a delicate child from a bourgeois family. He was active in Parisian high society during the 80s and 90s, welcomed in the most fashionable and exclusive salons of his day. However, his position there was also one of an outsider, due to his Jewishness and homosexuality. Towards the end of 1890s Proust began to withdraw more and more from society, and although he was never entirely reclusive, as is sometimes made out, he lapsed more completely into his lifelong tendency to sleep during the day and work at night. He was also plagued with severe asthma, which had troubled him intermittently since childhood, and a terror of his own death, especially in case it should come before his novel had been completed. The first volume, after some difficulty finding a publisher, came out in 1913, and Proust continued to work with an almost inhuman dedication on his masterpiece right up until his death in 1922, at the age of 51.<br /><br />Today he is widely recognised as one of the greatest authors of the 20th Century, and <i>À la recherche du temps perdu</i> as one of the most dazzling and significant works of literature to be written in modern times.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10303726357976908525" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/850515-sodome-et-gomorrhe">Quotes from Sodom and Gomorrah</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Then from those profound slumbers we awake in a dawn, not knowing who we are, being nobody, newly born, ready for anything, the brain emptied of that past which was life until then. And perhaps it is more wonderful still when our landing at the waking-point is abrupt and the thoughts of our sleep, hidden by a cloak of oblivion, have no time to return to us gradually, before sleep ceases. Then, from the black storm through which we seem to have passed (but we do not even say <i>we</i>), we emerge prostrate, without a thought, a <i>we</i> that is void of content.&rdquo;</span>
